    Creating the Rota • Combining Tools Using \'Iterator Algebra\'

    So, Yteria used itertools.cycle() to create two infinite iterators: one for tasks and the other for people. Note that the original lists, tasks and people, don\'t have the same number of elements.

    Next, Yteria needed to find a way to connect these two infinite iterators so that corresponding elements are matched. She needed a way to progress through the two infinite iterators at the same time. She needed something to "glue" them together…

    …or better still, to "zip" them together.

    This is where zip() comes in. The zip() built-in tool takes a number of iterators and zips them together, grouping the first elements of each iterator together, then grouping the second elements of each iterator together, and so on:

    #7

    And there it is. Remember that rota is an iterator since zip() returns an iterator. So, each time you fetch the next value from the rota iterator, you\'ll get a pairing between a person and the chore they need to do.

    Yteria finished this off with some quick code to display each day\'s rota. It would have been easier to use a for loop, but she couldn\'t. So she opted for this option, which is less tidy but still works:

    #8

    You can write the easier for loop version if you prefer. Note how Yteria, who\'s now proficient with the itertools module, also used itertools.count() to create a counter! She could have just created an integer and increment it each time, of course.

    Side note: The while loop above feels like something that could be implemented with the help of some itertools tools. Yteria felt this way, too. She wrote a note to try to refactor this while loop later, even if just as an exercise in playing with more of the tools in itertools. Do you want to have a go, too? If Yteria gets round to replacing this code, I\'ll let you know in a future post in The \'itertools\' Series.

    Here\'s the output from this code for the first few days:

    Press enter for the next day\'s rota...\nDay 1:\nIt\'s Yteria\'s turn to take the bins out\nIt\'s Silvia\'s turn to clean floor and carpets\nIt\'s Yteria\'s turn to wash up\n​\nPress enter for the next day\'s rota...\nDay 2:\nIt\'s Silvia\'s turn to take the bins out\nIt\'s Yteria\'s turn to clean floor and carpets\nIt\'s Silvia\'s turn to wash up\n​\nPress enter for the next day\'s rota...\nDay 3:\nIt\'s Yteria\'s turn to take the bins out\nIt\'s Silvia\'s turn to clean floor and carpets\nIt\'s Yteria\'s turn to wash up\n​\nPress enter for the next day\'s rota...\nDay 4:\nIt\'s Silvia\'s turn to take the bins out\nIt\'s Yteria\'s turn to clean floor and carpets\nIt\'s Silvia\'s turn to wash up\n​\nPress enter for the next day\'s rota...

    And of course, this code works with any number of tasks and any number of people.

    The itertools documentation page has a great line about combining various iteration tools using \'iterator algebra\'. Yteria\'s solution is an example of this. It combines two iteration tools, zip() and cycle(), to provide a neat solution. The tools in itertools are often useful as standalone tools. But they\'re even more powerful when you combine them with each other.

    Note that zip() and enumerate() aren\'t part of itertools since they\'re both built-in callables. However, they fall in the same category as the other tools in itertools—they\'re tools to help in particular iteration tasks.

    Support The Python Coding Stack

    Final Words

    Problem solved. Yteria and Silvia could now share the daily chores and make sure that everyone contributes equally. Yteria felt that her forced abstention from using the for keyword in Python led her to understand Pythonic iteration a lot better. She felt like an iteration pro now! Iterators are at the heart of iteration in Python. And itertools provides lots of useful iterators.

    def create_infinite_sequence(sequence):\n    index = 0\n    while True:\n        yield sequence[index]\n        index = 0 if index == len(sequence) - 1 else index + 1\n
    Code Block #5
    tasks_inf_seq = create_infinite_sequence(tasks)\n\nnext(tasks_inf_seq)\n# \'Take the bins out\'\nnext(tasks_inf_seq)\n# \'Clean floor and carpets\'\nnext(tasks_inf_seq)\n# \'Wash up\'\nnext(tasks_inf_seq)\n# \'Take the bins out\'\nnext(tasks_inf_seq)\n# \'Clean floor and carpets\'\nnext(tasks_inf_seq)\n# \'Wash up\'\n
    Code Block #6
    import itertools\nimport timeit\n\ntasks = ["Take the bins out", "Clean floor and carpets", "Wash up"]\npeople = ["Yteria", "Silvia"]\n\ndef create_infinite_sequence(sequence):\n    index = 0\n    while True:\n        yield sequence[index]\n        index = 0 if index == len(sequence) - 1 else index + 1\n\ninfinite_tasks = create_infinite_sequence(tasks)\ninfinite_tasks_cyc = itertools.cycle(tasks)\n\nprint(\n    "Using \'create_infinite_sequence()\':\\n",\n    timeit.timeit(\n        "next(infinite_tasks)",\n        number=10_000_000,\n        globals=globals(),\n    )\n)\n\nprint(\n    "Using \'itertools.cycle()\':\\n",\n    timeit.timeit(\n        "next(infinite_tasks_cyc)",\n        number=10_000_000,\n        globals=globals(),\n    )\n)\n

    import itertools\n\ntasks = ["Take the bins out", "Clean floor and carpets", "Wash up"]\npeople = ["Yteria", "Silvia"]\n\nrota = zip(\n    itertools.cycle(people),\n    itertools.cycle(tasks),\n)\n
    Code Block #8
    import itertools\n\ntasks = ["Take the bins out", "Clean floor and carpets", "Wash up"]\npeople = ["Yteria", "Silvia"]\n\nrota = zip(\n    itertools.cycle(people),\n    itertools.cycle(tasks),\n)\n\nday_counter = itertools.count(start=1)\nwhile True:\n    input("\\nPress enter for the next day\'s rota...")\n    day = next(day_counter)\n    print(f"Day {day}:")\n    # The next bit would be easier using a \'for\' loop,\n    # but Yteria couldn\'t do this!\n    while True:\n        person, task = next(rota)\n        print(f"It\'s {person}\'s turn to {task.lower()}")\n        if task == tasks[-1]:\n            break\n

    Nikola

    Nikola v8.3.3 is out!

    \n

    \n

    On behalf of the Nikola team, I am pleased to announce\nthe immediate availability of Nikola v8.3.3. This is a bugfix release.

    \n

    We\xe2\x80\x99ve had to release Nikola v8.3.3 immediately after releasing\nNikola v8.3.2, as it is broken on Python 3.8. We would like to thank\nthe Python packaging ecosystem for being an incomprehensible and\nincompatible trainwreck.

    \n\n

    What is Nikola?

    \n

    Nikola is a static site and blog generator, written in Python.\nIt can use Mako and Jinja2 templates, and input in many popular markup\nformats, such as reStructuredText and Markdown \xe2\x80\x94 and can even turn\nJupyter Notebooks into blog posts! It also supports image galleries,\nand is multilingual. Nikola is flexible, and page builds are extremely\nfast, courtesy of doit (which is rebuilding only what has been changed).

    \n

    Find out more at the website: https://getnikola.com/

    \n\n\n

    Downloads

    \n

    Install using pip install Nikola.

    \n\n\n

    Changes from v8.3.1

    \n\n

    Features

    \n
      \n

    • Trace template usage when an environment variable NIKOLA_TEMPLATES_TRACE\nis set to any non-empty value.

      • \n

    • Give user control over the raw underlying template engine\n(either mako.lookup.TemplateLookup or jinja2.Environment)\nvia an optional conf.py method TEMPLATE_ENGINE_FACTORY.

      • \n

    • Switch to pyproject.toml

      • \n

    • Add path handler slug_source linking to source of post.

      • \n
    \n\n\n

    Bugfixes

    \n
      \n

    • Ignore errors in parsing SVG files for shrinking them, copy original file to output instead\n(Issue #3785)

      • \n

    • Restore annotation_helper.tmpl with dummy content - fix themes still mentioning it\n(Issue #3764, #3773)

      • \n

    • Fix compatibility with watchdog 4 (Issue #3766)

      • \n

    • nikola serve now works with non-root SITE_URL.

      • \n

    • Stack traces meaningless for end users now more reliably suppressed (Issue #3838).

      • \n
    \n\n\n

    Other

    \n
      \n

    • Changed filter for tidy from tidy5 to tidy.

      • \n

    PyCon

    \n\n\n

    Welcoming 8 Companies to Startup Row at PyCon US 2025

    \n

    \n

    PyCon US gives the community a chance to come together and learn about what\xe2\x80\x99s new and interesting about the Python language and the seemingly infinite variety of problems that can be solved with a few (or a few thousand) lines of Python code. For entrepreneurial Pythonistas, Startup Row at PyCon US presents a unique opportunity for startup companies to connect directly with the developer community they\xe2\x80\x99re building for.

    Kicked off in 2011, Startup Row at PyCon US gives early-stage startups access to the best of what PyCon US has to offer, including conference passes and booth space, at no cost to their teams. Since its inception, including this year\xe2\x80\x99s batch, well over 150 companies have been featured on Startup Row, and there\xe2\x80\x99s a good chance you are familiar with some of their products and projects. Pandas, Modin, Codon, Ludwig, Horovod, SLSA, and dozens of other open-source tools were built or commercialized by companies featured on Startup Row at PyCon US.

    Think of Startup Row at PyCon US as a peek into the future of the Python software ecosystem. And with that, we\xe2\x80\x99re pleased to introduce the 2025 batch!

    The Startup Row 2025 Lineup

    AgentOps

    Building an AI agent that works is only half the battle; seeing why it fails, how much it costs, and whether it\xe2\x80\x99s about to go rogue is the other half. AgentOps gives developers that missing x-ray vision. Drop a two-line SDK into your code and every run is captured as a \xe2\x80\x9csession\xe2\x80\x9d complete with step-by-step waterfalls, prompt/response pairs, cost and token metrics, and security flags\xe2\x80\x94instantly viewable in a web dashboard.

    The idea was born at a San Francisco hackathon, where co-founders Alex Reibman and Adam Silverman discovered that their agent-debugging tools were more popular than the agents themselves. They turned those internal tools into AgentOps, raised a $2.6 million pre-seed led by 645 Ventures and Afore Capital in August 2024, and now give thousands of AI engineers a live dashboard that replays every agent step, surfaces exact cost and latency metrics, and enforces benchmark-driven safety checks\xe2\x80\x94all from a two-line SDK.

    Open-sourced under an MIT license, the project has already racked up 4.4k GitHub stars and integrates out-of-the-box with OpenAI Agents SDK, CrewAI, LangChain, AutoGen and dozens of other frameworks. With observability handled, AgentOps wants to be to autonomous agents what Datadog is to micro-services: the layer that makes ambitious agent stacks safe enough for production\xe2\x80\x94and cheap enough to keep running.

    AllHands AI

    Agentic coding went from a theoretical possibility to something seemingly overnight, and All Hands AI\xe2\x80\x99s open-source platform OpenHands is one of the reasons why. Written in Python (with a JavaScript front-end), OpenHands lets an AI developer do everything a human can: edit repositories, run shell commands, browse the web, call APIs\xe2\x80\x94even lift snippets straight from Stack Overflow\xe2\x80\x94and then roll it all back into a commit you can review and merge.

    Since its first README-only commit just 14 months ago, the project has snowballed into 54k-plus GitHub stars and 6k forks, backed by a community of roughly 310 contributors and counting. The momentum helped the team close a $5 million seed round led by Menlo Ventures last September, giving the ten-person startup runway to layer commercial tooling on top of its permissively-licensed core.

    \xe2\x80\x9cAbout six months ago it finally clicked\xe2\x80\x94I now write about 95% of my own code with agents,\xe2\x80\x9d says co-founder and chief scientist Graham Neubig, an associate professor at Carnegie Mellon who shipped the project\xe2\x80\x99s first lines before Robert Brennan\xe2\x80\x94now CEO\xe2\x80\x94joined the project and built a globally-distributed team to scale it up. Neubig credits the early decision to ship a \xe2\x80\x9cnon-functional prototype\xe2\x80\x9d and build in public for catalyzing the contributor base; today, community members maintain everything from Windows support to protocol bridges while swapping LLM benchmarks daily in the project\xe2\x80\x99s Slack.

    OpenHands has evolved from a weekend proof-of-concept into a community-driven framework that now aims for production-grade reliability as an open alternative to proprietary code agents. Weekly releases focus on reproducible debugging, cost control, and enterprise safeguards, and contributors are already using the system to generate and review real pull requests across a growing set of Python projects.

    DiffStudio

    Product photos tell a story, but DiffStudio wants to let shoppers walk around that story. The North-Jersey startup is building a camera-agnostic \xe2\x80\x9cinverse graphics\xe2\x80\x9d pipeline that ingests a handful of ordinary 2-D shots or video and returns a fully-textured, web-ready 3-D model that drops into any product page. The goal is simple: turn scrolling into spinning, pinching, and zooming\xe2\x80\x94and watch engagement and conversions rise.

    Founder Naga Karumuri just recently formed the company in December, after months of hacking on the latest developments in Gaussian splatting and differentiable rendering. \xe2\x80\x9cYou upload a batch of images, and our model hands you a compressed asset\xe2\x80\x94think megabytes, not gigabytes\xe2\x80\x94that Shopify can serve instantly,\xe2\x80\x9d Karumuri explained. A companion mobile app will let merchants scan products on the fly, while a web dashboard handles cloud processing and one-click embeds.

    DiffStudio\xe2\x80\x99s beachhead market is small- and mid-sized Shopify sellers, and blue-chip retailers are already circling. \xe2\x80\x9cIn casual chats we\xe2\x80\x99ve had interest from brands like Adidas and Michael Kors,\xe2\x80\x9d Karumuri noted, hinting at an eventual move up-market once the self-service MVP launches. Compression and quality are the differentiators: where existing tools like Polycam focus on hobbyist scans or LiDAR-assisted captures, DiffStudio is chasing photo-real fidelity with file sizes that won\xe2\x80\x99t tank page speed. The project\xe2\x80\x99s GitHub repositories showcase early demos and the startup\xe2\x80\x99s open-source commitment.

    The team is still lean\xe2\x80\x94Karumuri plus a collaborator\xe2\x80\x94but the vision is outsized: make 3-D product \xe2\x80\x9cdigital twins\xe2\x80\x9d as easy to generate as a product photo set. Or, as their LinkedIn banner puts it, \xe2\x80\x9cSplat your products into 3D glory.\xe2\x80\x9d

    Fabi.ai

    Business users shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have to ping the data team for every ad-hoc question\xe2\x80\x94and data scientists shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t spend half their day writing the same queries on repeat. Fabi.ai positions itself as the AI \xe2\x80\x9cside-kick\xe2\x80\x9d that lets both camps meet in the middle: a web notebook where natural-language prompts, SQL, Python, and no-code building blocks live side-by-side, with generative agents filling in (and explaining) 90 % of the boilerplate.

    Founded in 2023 and headquartered in the San-Francisco Bay Area, the six-person team works face-to-face in San Mateo to iterate quickly on the product. CEO Marc Dupuis ran embedded analytics at revenue-ops unicorn Clari before teaming up again with former colleague Lei Tang (now CTO) to \xe2\x80\x9clet vibe-coders do 95 % of their own analysis\xe2\x80\x9d while still giving experts an easy way to supervise the last mile.

    Eniac Ventures and Outlander VC co-led a $3 million seed round in July 2023 to bring Fabi.ai\xe2\x80\x99s collaborative notebook to market. Early customers already range from fast growing startups to established e-commerce brands.

    With BI dashboards stuck on the what and legacy notebooks siloed on individual laptops, Fabi.ai is betting that a cloud-native, agent-augmented workspace is the missing link\xe2\x80\x94and it\xe2\x80\x99s inviting the Python community to kick the tires (and write fewer queries) at PyCon US.

    Gooey.ai

    Most no-code AI builders stop at slick demos; Gooey.ai is obsessed with what happens after the hype, when a multilingual chatbot has to work for a Kenyan farmer with a 2 G signal or a frontline nurse switching between English and Kannada. The open-source, low-code platform stitches together the \xe2\x80\x9cbest of private and public AI\xe2\x80\x9d into reusable workflows\xe2\x80\x94text, speech, vision and RAG pipelines you can fork, remix and ship to WhatsApp, SMS, Slack or the web from a single dashboard. One billing account, one-click deploy.

    Founders Sean Blagsvedt (ex-Microsoft Research, founder of Indian job-matching startup Babajob), Archana Prasad (artist-turned-social-tech entrepreneur), and CTO Dev Aggarwal split their time between Seattle and Bangalore and run the company under the umbrella of Dara Network. Their thesis: impactful AI needs to be both affordable and local\xe2\x80\x94so Gooey bakes in speech recognition for low-resource languages, translation APIs like India\xe2\x80\x99s Bhashini, and zero-data-retention options for NGOs handling sensitive chats.

    Real-world traction is already visible. An agronomy WhatsApp bot built on Gooey reached \xe2\x80\x9ctens of thousands of farmers in Kenya, India, Ethiopia and Rwanda,\xe2\x80\x9d delivering accurate, objective answers with page-level citations. The platform\xe2\x80\x99s copilot builder now supports the latest GPT-4o, Llama 3, Claude, Gemini and Mistral models; integrates OCR, vision and text-to-speech; and ships bulk evaluation harnesses so teams can test new prompts before they hit production.

    To seed more grassroots projects, Gooey recently launched a Workflow Accelerator with funding from The Rockefeller Foundation, covering model and SMS costs for six NGOs and open-sourcing every workflow that emerges. If you\xe2\x80\x99re looking to take an AI pilot from \xe2\x80\x9ccool demo\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9c24/7 field tool,\xe2\x80\x9d Gooey.ai wants to be the glue\xe2\x80\x94and the infra\xe2\x80\x94you won\xe2\x80\x99t outgrow.

    GripTape AI

    Enterprise AI teams love the idea of autonomous agents, but hate the roulette wheel of prompt-only code. Griptape steps in with a Python framework that keeps creativity where it belongs\xe2\x80\x94inside LLM calls\xe2\x80\x94while wrapping every outside step in predictable, testable software patterns. Agents, sequential pipelines, and parallel workflows are first-class \xe2\x80\x9cStructures\xe2\x80\x9d; memory, rulesets, and observability are plug-in Drivers; and an \xe2\x80\x9cOff-Prompt\xe2\x80\x9d mechanism pushes sensitive or bulky data out of the prompt for lower cost and higher security.

    The project launched in early 2023 and has already gathered \xe2\x89\x882.3 k GitHub stars and an active Discord community. Adoption accelerated after co-founders Kyle Roche and Vasily Vasinov\xe2\x80\x94both former AWS leaders\xe2\x80\x94closed a $12.5 million Seed Round in September 2023 led by Seattle\xe2\x80\x99s FUSE and Acequia Capital. The fresh capital funds Griptape Cloud, a fully managed runtime that hosts ETL pipelines, hybrid vector knowledge bases, and structure executions while piping metrics to whatever monitoring stack a Fortune 500 already uses.

    Under the Apache-2.0 license, developers can start locally, swap between OpenAI, Bedrock or Anthropic drivers, and graduate to the cloud only when they need auto-scaling or policy enforcement. In short, Griptape aims to be the Django of agentic AI: batteries-included, prod-ready, and with enough guardrails that even the compliance team can sleep at night.

    Griptape also recently launched Griptape Nodes, an intuitive, drag-and-drop interface where designers, artists and other creative professionals can create advanced creative pipelines using graphs, nodes, and flowcharts to exploit state-of-the-art image generation and image processing models, together with more \xe2\x80\x9ctraditional\xe2\x80\x9d large language models.

    MLJAR

    Most AutoML platforms lock you into a browser tab and someone else\xe2\x80\x99s GPU cluster. MLJAR takes the opposite approach: everything runs locally, yet you still get the \xe2\x80\x9ctrain, explain, and deploy\xe2\x80\x9d cycle in a single click.

    The Polish-based project began in 2016, when founder Piotr P\xc5\x82o\xc5\x84ski\xe2\x80\x94fresh from a PhD spent building models for physicists, bioinformaticians, and telecom giants\xe2\x80\x94decided he was tired of rewriting the same pipelines over and over. Impatience, not laziness, pushed him to automate the entire workflow.

    Today the three-person team (Piotr, his co-founder wife, and a close friend) maintains a fully open-source stack. The flagship MLJAR-AutoML package handles feature engineering, hyper-parameter search, and rich Markdown reports; Mercury turns any Jupyter notebook into a shareable web app or dashboard with a sprinkle of widgets; and the brand-new MLJAR Studio Desktop app bundles its own Python environment, point-and-click \xe2\x80\x9ccode recipes,\xe2\x80\x9d an integrated GPT-4 assistant, and a one-button Share that converts a notebook into a live web application.

    Open source is more than a distribution strategy\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s a trust signal. One recognisable enterprise adopted the package under an MIT license and then contracted the team for advanced features such as fairness-aware training. Revenue is a side effect; the primary goal is software that makes data science faster, friendlier, and fully under the user\xe2\x80\x99s control.

    If you\xe2\x80\x99ve ever wished Streamlit met AutoML\xe2\x80\x94and ran natively on your laptop\xe2\x80\x94swing by the MLJAR booth on Startup Row at PyCon US and take Studio for a spin.

    Ragas

    Seemingly everyone is building RAG pipelines, but almost no one is measuring them. Ragas sets out to be \xe2\x80\x9cpytest for Retrieval-Augmented Generation,\xe2\x80\x9d bundling ready-made metrics\xe2\x80\x94context recall, faithfulness, answer relevancy\xe2\x80\x94and auto-generated test sets so teams can turn vibe checks into repeatable CI tests. Drop the library into LangChain, LlamaIndex, or plain-Python code and Ragas spits out a single \xe2\x80\x9cRagas Score\xe2\x80\x9d (plus per-metric drill-downs) that tracks whether your latest prompt tweak fixed accuracy or broke it.

    The project landed a shout-out during OpenAI\xe2\x80\x99s Dev Day and has since snowballed to 9.1 k GitHub stars and 900+ forks, with more than 80 external contributors. In production it now processes ~5 million evaluations a month for engineers at AWS, Microsoft, Databricks, and Moody\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94a number growing 70 % month-over-month.

    Co-founders Jithin James (early engineer at BentoML) and Shahul ES (Kaggle Grandmaster, core contributor to Open-Assistant) met at college, hacked on open-source together for years, and entered Y Combinator\xe2\x80\x99s W24 batch to turn their weekend project into a commercial platform. Their plan: keep the core evaluator MIT-licensed while DG Labs, the commercial arm, layers team dashboards, experiment tracking, and dataset management on top\xe2\x80\x94so every product squad can ship RAG updates with CI-style confidence.

    Thank You\xe2\x80\x99s and Acknowledgements

    There are far too many stakeholders in the ongoing success of Startup Row at PyCon US to name individually, but this program would not be possible without the following folks:
    • The Python Software Foundation, for its continued support of this little corner of PyCon US.
    • The PSF Sponsorship team, for managing the logistics of getting everyone registered and set up for success
    • Startup Row co-organizers, Jason D. Rowley (p.s. hey, that\'s me!) and and collaborator, Shea Tate-Di Donna, whose first experience with the Python community was presenting her company, Zana, on Startup Row at PyCon US 2015.
    • Startup Row alumni companies that come back as paid sponsors at PyCon US. Shoutouts to Anvil (SR\xe2\x80\x9917), Chainguard (SR\xe2\x80\x9922), and Dagster (SR\xe2\x80\x9921), whose support helps make Startup Row at PyCon US possible.
    • To all startup founders who filled out the (mercifully brief) application. To those that did not get a spot this year, we appreciate your time and attention. To those that did: a hearty congratulations.
    • To the selection committee, for accomplishing the difficult task of evaluating and scoring applications.
    2026 will mark 15 years of Startup Row at PyCon US. We can\'t wait to see what\'s next in the Python software ecosystem. \xf0\x9f\x90\x8d

    Daniel Roy Greenfeld

    \n\n\n

    Farewell to Michael Ryabushkin

    \n

    \n

    Michael Ryabushkin and I met around 2011-2012 through Python community work. I don\'t remember how we met, instead I remember his presence suddenly there, helping and aiding others.

    \n

    Michael could be pushy. He was trying to help people reach their full potential. His energy and humor was relentless, I admired his tenacity and giving nature.

    \n

    While our coding preferences usually clashed, sometimes they matched. Then we would rant together about some tiny detail, those talks plus the silly Tai Chi dance we did are lovely memories I have of Michael.

    \n

    In 2016 my wife Audrey had emergency surgery. For me that meant sleepless days taking care of her. Suddenly Michael\'s presence was there. He took shifts, ran errands (including buying a wheelchair), and forced me to sleep. I am forever grateful to Michael for what he did for us.

    \n

    In early 2020 Audrey and I got last minute approval to use a large conference space to organize an event called PyBeach. Michael heard about it and as always, suddenly his presence was there. He was not just a volunteer at large, but leading the conference with us. Michael and I had our shared code rants, did our silly Tai Chi dance, and he met our baby daughter.

    \n

    Between the pandemic and us moving from the Los Angeles area I didn\'t get the chance to see Michael again. I\'ll miss our rants, our silly Tai Chi dance, and his sudden appearances.

    \n

    \n

    SoCal Python has created a memorial page in Michael\'s honor.

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    Brett Cannon

    \n\n\n

    Unravelling t-strings

    \n

    \n

    PEP 750 introduced t-strings for Python 3.14. In fact, they are so new that as of Python 3.14.0b1 there still isn&apost any documentation yet for t-strings. 😅 As such, this blog post will hopefully help explain what exactly t-strings are and what you might use them for by unravelling the syntax and briefly talking about potential uses for t-strings.

    What are they?

    I like to think of t-strings as a syntactic way to expose the parser used for f-strings. I&aposll explain later what that might be useful for, but for now let&aposs see exactly what t-strings unravel into.

    Let&aposs start with an example by trying to use t-strings to mostly replicate f-strings. We will define a function named f_yeah() which takes a t-string and returns what it would be formatted had it been an f-string (e.g. f"{42}" == f_yeah(t"{42}")). Here is the example we will be working with and slowly refining:

    def f_yeah(t_string):\n    """Convert a t-string into what an f-string would have provided."""\n    return t_string\n\n\nif __name__ == "__main__":\n    name = "world"\n    expected = f"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!"\n    actual = f_yeah(expected)\n\n    assert actual == expected

    As of right now, f_yeah() is just the identity function which takes the actual result of an f-string, which is pretty boring and useless. So let&aposs parse what the t-string would be into its constituent parts:

    def f_yeah(t_string):\n    """Convert a t-string into what an f-string would have provided."""\n    return "".join(t_string)\n\n\nif __name__ == "__main__":\n    name = "world"\n    expected = f"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!"\n    parsed = [\n        "Hello, ",\n        "world",\n        "! Conversions like ",\n        "&aposworld&apos",\n        " and format specs like ",\n        "world ",\n        " work!",\n    ]\n    actual = f_yeah(parsed)\n\n    assert actual == expected

    Here we have split the f-string output into a list of the string parts that make it up, joining it all together with "".join(). This is actually what the bytecode for f-strings does once it has converted everything in the replacement fields – i.e. what&aposs in the curly braces – into strings.

    But this is still not that interesting. We can definitely parse out more information.

    def f_yeah(t_string):\n    """Convert a t-string into what an f-string would have provided."""\n    return "".join(t_string)\n\n\nif __name__ == "__main__":\n    name = "world"\n    expected = f"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!"\n    parsed = [\n        "Hello, ",\n        name,\n        "! Conversions like ",\n        repr(name),\n        " and format specs like ",\n        format(name, "<6"),\n        " work!",\n    ]\n    actual = f_yeah(parsed)\n\n    assert actual == expected

    Now we have substituted the string literals we had for the replacement fields with what Python does behind the scenes with conversions like !r and format specs like :<6. As you can see, there are effectively three parts to handling a replacement field:

    1. Evaluating the Python expression
    2. Applying any specified conversion (let&aposs say the default is None)
    3. Applying any format spec (let&aposs say the default is "")

    So let&aposs get our "parser" to separate all of that out for us into a tuple of 3 items: value, conversion, and format spec. That way we can have our f_yeah() function handle the actual formatting of the replacement fields.

    def f_yeah(t_string):\n    """Convert a t-string into what an f-string would have provided."""\n    converters = {func.__name__[0]: func for func in (str, repr, ascii)}\n    converters[None] = str\n\n    parts = []\n    for part in t_string:\n        match part:\n            case (value, conversion, format_spec):\n                parts.append(format(converters[conversion](value), format_spec))\n            case str():\n                parts.append(part)\n\n    return "".join(parts)\n\n\nif __name__ == "__main__":\n    name = "world"\n    expected = f"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!"\n    parsed = [\n        "Hello, ",\n        (name, None, ""),\n        "! Conversions like ",\n        (name, "r", ""),\n        " and format specs like ",\n        (name, None, "<6"),\n        " work!",\n    ]\n    actual = f_yeah(parsed)\n\n    assert actual == expected\n

    Now we have f_yeah() taking the value from the expression of the replacement field, applying the appropriate conversion, and then passing that on to format(). This gives us a more useful parsed representation! Since we have the string representation of the expression, we might as well just keep that around even if we don&apost use it in our example (parsers typically don&apost like to throw information away).

    def f_yeah(t_string):\n    """Convert a t-string into what an f-string would have provided."""\n    converters = {func.__name__[0]: func for func in (str, repr, ascii)}\n    converters[None] = str\n\n    parts = []\n    for part in t_string:\n        match part:\n            case (value, _, conversion, format_spec):\n                parts.append(format(converters[conversion](value), format_spec))\n            case str():\n                parts.append(part)\n\n    return "".join(parts)\n\n\nif __name__ == "__main__":\n    name = "world"\n    expected = f"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!"\n    parsed = [\n        "Hello, ",\n        (name, "name", None, ""),\n        "! Conversions like ",\n        (name, "name", "r", ""),\n        " and format specs like ",\n        (name, "name", None, "<6"),\n        " work!",\n    ]\n    actual = f_yeah(parsed)\n\n    assert actual == expected

    The next thing we want our parsed output to be is be a bit easier to work with. A 4-item tuple is a bit unwieldy, so let&aposs define a class named Interpolation that will hold all the relevant details of the replacement field.

    class Interpolation:\n    __match_args__ = ("value", "expression", "conversion", "format_spec")\n\n    def __init__(\n        self,\n        value,\n        expression,\n        conversion=None,\n        format_spec="",\n    ):\n        self.value = value\n        self.expression = expression\n        self.conversion = conversion\n        self.format_spec = format_spec\n\n\ndef f_yeah(t_string):\n    """Convert a t-string into what an f-string would have provided."""\n    converters = {func.__name__[0]: func for func in (str, repr, ascii)}\n    converters[None] = str\n\n    parts = []\n    for part in t_string:\n        match part:\n            case Interpolation(value, _, conversion, format_spec):\n                parts.append(format(converters[conversion](value), format_spec))\n            case str():\n                parts.append(part)\n\n    return "".join(parts)\n\n\nif __name__ == "__main__":\n    name = "world"\n    expected = f"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!"\n    parsed = [\n        "Hello, ",\n        Interpolation(name, "name"),\n        "! Conversions like ",\n        Interpolation(name, "name", "r"),\n        " and format specs like ",\n        Interpolation(name, "name", format_spec="<6"),\n        " work!",\n    ]\n    actual = f_yeah(parsed)\n\n    assert actual == expected

    That&aposs better! Now we have an object-oriented structure to our parsed replacement field, which is easier to work with than the 4-item tuple we had before. We can also extend this object-oriented organization to the list we have been using to hold all the parsed data.

    class Interpolation:\n    __match_args__ = ("value", "expression", "conversion", "format_spec")\n\n    def __init__(\n        self,\n        value,\n        expression,\n        conversion=None,\n        format_spec="",\n    ):\n        self.value = value\n        self.expression = expression\n        self.conversion = conversion\n        self.format_spec = format_spec\n\n\nclass Template:\n    def __init__(self, *args):\n        # There will always be N+1 strings for N interpolations;\n        # that may mean inserting an empty string at the start or end.\n        strings = []\n        interpolations = []\n        if args and isinstance(args[0], Interpolation):\n            strings.append("")\n        for arg in args:\n            match arg:\n                case str():\n                    strings.append(arg)\n                case Interpolation():\n                    interpolations.append(arg)\n        if args and isinstance(args[-1], Interpolation):\n            strings.append("")\n\n        self._iter = args\n        self.strings = tuple(strings)\n        self.interpolations = tuple(interpolations)\n\n    @property\n    def values(self):\n        return tuple(interpolation.value for interpolation in self.interpolations)\n\n    def __iter__(self):\n        return iter(self._iter)\n\n\ndef f_yeah(t_string):\n    """Convert a t-string into what an f-string would have provided."""\n    converters = {func.__name__[0]: func for func in (str, repr, ascii)}\n    converters[None] = str\n\n    parts = []\n    for part in t_string:\n        match part:\n            case Interpolation(value, _, conversion, format_spec):\n                parts.append(format(converters[conversion](value), format_spec))\n            case str():\n                parts.append(part)\n\n    return "".join(parts)\n\n\nif __name__ == "__main__":\n    name = "world"\n    expected = f"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!"\n    parsed = Template(\n        "Hello, ",\n        Interpolation(name, "name"),\n        "! Conversions like ",\n        Interpolation(name, "name", "r"),\n        " and format specs like ",\n        Interpolation(name, "name", format_spec="<6"),\n        " work!",\n    )\n    actual = f_yeah(parsed)\n\n    assert actual == expected

    And that&aposs t-strings! We parsed f"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!" into Template("Hello, ", Interpolation(name, "name"), "! Conversions like ", Interpolation(name, "name", "r"), " and format specs like ", Interpolation(name, "name", format_spec="<6")," work!"). We were then able to use our f_yeah() function to convert the t-string into what an equivalent f-string would have looked like. The actual code to use to test this in Python 3.14 with an actual t-string is the following (PEP 750 has its own version of converting a t-string to an f-string which greatly inspired my example):

    from string import templatelib\n\n\ndef f_yeah(t_string):\n    """Convert a t-string into what an f-string would have provided."""\n    converters = {func.__name__[0]: func for func in (str, repr, ascii)}\n    converters[None] = str\n\n    parts = []\n    for part in t_string:\n        match part:\n            case templatelib.Interpolation(value, _, conversion, format_spec):\n                parts.append(format(converters[conversion](value), format_spec))\n            case str():\n                parts.append(part)\n\n    return "".join(parts)\n\n\nif __name__ == "__main__":\n    name = "world"\n    expected = f"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!"\n    parsed = t"Hello, {name}! Conversions like {name!r} and format specs like {name:<6} work!"\n    actual = f_yeah(parsed)\n\n    assert actual == expected

    What are t-strings good for?

    As I mentioned earlier, I view t-strings as a syntactic way to get access to the f-string parser. So, what do you usually use a parser with? The stereotypical thing is compiling something. Since we are dealing with strings here, what are some common strings you "compile"? The most common answer are things like SQL statements and HTML: things that require some processing of what you pass into a template to make sure something isn&apost going to go awry. That suggests that you could have a sql() function that takes a t-string and compiles a SQL statement that avoids SQL injection attacks. Same goes for HTML and JavaScript injection attacks.

    Add in logging and you get the common examples. But I suspect that the community is going to come up with some interesting uses of t-strings and their parsed data (e.g. PEP 787 and using t-strings to create the arguments to subprocess.run())!

    \n

    Our new accessibility statement

    \n

    \n

    Happy Global Accessibility Awareness Day! We thought this would be a fitting occasion to announce our brand new Django accessibility statement \xf0\x9f\x8e\x89

    \n

    Did you know that according to the WebAIM Million survey, 94.6% of sites have easily-detectable accessibility issues? We all need to work together to build a more inclusive web (also check out our diversity statement if you haven\xe2\x80\x99t already!). There are accessibility gaps in Django itself too. This statement improves transparency, and clearly states our intentions. And we hope it encourages our community and the industry at large to more widely consider accessibility.

    \n

    How to use this statement

    \n

    Read it, share it with your friends, or in a procurement context!

    \n
      \n
    • Use it to understand where there are gaps in Django that need to be addressed on projects.
      • \n
    • And opportunities to contribute to Django and related projects \xe2\x9d\xa4\xef\xb8\x8f
      • \n
    • Factor it into legal compliance. For example with the European Accessibility Act. Starting June 2025, accessibility becomes a legal requirement for large swaths of the private sector in the European Union.
      • \n
    • Share it with venues for Django events to demonstrate the importance of accessibility for their competitiveness.
      • \n
    \n

    How you can help

    \n

    Take a moment to provide any feedback you might have about the statement on the Django Forum. Let us know if you would prefer additional reporting like an ATAG audit, or VPAT, ACR, or any other acronym. Let us know if you\xe2\x80\x99d like to contribute to the accessibility of the Django community! \xf0\x9f\xab\xb6

    \n

    \nMay 15, 2025 02:00 PM UTC\n

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    Ned Batchelder

    \n\n\n

    PyCon summer camp

    \n

    \n

    I’m headed to PyCon today, and I’m reminded about how it feels like summer\ncamp, in mostly good ways, but also in a tricky way.

    You take some time off from your “real” life, you go somewhere else, you hang\nout with old friends and meet some new friends. You do different things than in\nyour real life, some are playful, some take real work. These are all good ways\nit’s like summer camp.

    Here’s the tricky thing to watch out for: like summer camp, you can make\nconnections to people or projects that are intense and feel like they could last\nforever. You make friends at summer camp, or even have semi-romantic crushes on\npeople. You promise to stay in touch, you think it’s the “real thing.” When you\nget home, you write an email or two, maybe a phone call, but it fades away. The\nexcitement of the summer is overtaken by your autumnal real life again.

    PyCon can be the same way, either with people or projects. Not a romance,\nbut the exciting feeling that you want to keep doing the project you started at\nPyCon, or be a member of some community you hung out with for those days. You\nwant to keep talking about that exciting thing with that person. These are\ngreat feelings, but it’s easy to emotionally over-commit to those efforts and\nthen have it fade away once PyCon is over.

    How do you know what projects are just crushes, and which are permanent\nrelationships? Maybe it doesn’t matter, and we should just get excited about\nthings.

    I know I started at least one effort last year that I thought would be done\nin a few months, but has since stalled. Now I am headed back to PyCon. Will I\nbecome attached to yet more things this time? Is that bad? Should I temper my\nenthusiasm, or is it fine to light a few fires and accept that some will peter\nout?

    \n

    \nMay 15, 2025 11:05 AM UTC\n

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    Zato Blog

    \n\n\n

    Using Oracle Database from Python and Zato Services

    \n

    \n

    \n Using Oracle Database from Python and Zato Services\n

    \n
    \n 2025-05-15, by Dariusz Suchojad\n\n
    \n\n

    Overview

    \n

    Oracle Database remains a cornerstone of enterprise IT, powering mission-critical applications around the world.\nIntegrating Oracle with Python unlocks automation, reporting, and API-based workflows. In this article, you\'ll learn how to:

    \n
      \n
    • Connect to Oracle Database from Python
      • \n
    • Use Oracle Database in Zato services
      • \n
    • Execute SQL queries and call stored procedures
      • \n
    • Understand the underlying SQL objects
      • \n
    \n

    All examples are based on real-world use cases and follow best practices for security and maintainability.

    \n

    Why Use Oracle Database from Python?

    \n

    Python is a popular language for automation, integration, and data processing. By connecting Python to Oracle Database, you can:

    \n
      \n
    • Automate business processes
      • \n
    • Build APIs that interact with enterprise data
      • \n
    • Run analytics and reporting jobs
      • \n
    • Integrate with other systems using Zato
      • \n
    \n

    Using Oracle Database in Zato Services

    \n

    SQL connections are configured in the Dashboard, and you can use them directly in your service code.

    \n

    In all the service below, the logic is split into several dedicated services,\neach responsible for a specific operation. This separation improves clarity, reusability, and maintainability.

    \n

    \n

    Setting Up: Oracle Database Objects

    \n

    First, let\'s start with the basic SQL objects used in our examples:

    \n
    -- Table definition\nCREATE TABLE users (\n    user_id NUMBER,\n    username VARCHAR2(50)\n);\n
    \n
    -- Sample data\nINSERT INTO users (user_id, username) VALUES (1, \'john_doe\');\nINSERT INTO users (user_id, username) VALUES (2, \'jane_smith\');\nINSERT INTO users (user_id, username) VALUES (3, \'bob_jones\');\n
    \n
    -- Stored procedure: process_data\nCREATE OR REPLACE PROCEDURE process_data (\n    input_num   IN  NUMBER,\n    input_str   IN  VARCHAR2,\n    output_num  OUT NUMBER,\n    output_str  OUT VARCHAR2\n)\nAS\nBEGIN\n    output_num := input_num * 2;\n    output_str := \'Input was: \' || input_str;\nEND process_data;\n/\n
    \n
    -- Stored procedure: get_users\nCREATE OR REPLACE PROCEDURE get_users (\n    recordset OUT SYS_REFCURSOR\n)\nAS\nBEGIN\n    OPEN recordset FOR\n        SELECT user_id, username\n        FROM users\n        ORDER BY user_id;\nEND get_users;\n/\n
    \n

    1. Querying All Users

    \n

    This service retrieves all users from the users table.

    \n
    # -*- coding: utf-8 -*-\nfrom zato.server.service import Service\n\nclass GetAllUsers(Service):\n    """ Service to retrieve all users from the database.\n    """\n    def handle(self):\n\n        # Obtain a reference to the configured Oracle Database connection\n        conn = self.out.sql[\'My Oracle DB\']\n\n        # Define the SQL query to select all rows from the users table\n        query = \'select * from users\'\n\n        # Execute the query; returns a list of dictionaries, one per row\n        response = conn.execute(query)\n\n        # Set the service response to the query result\n        self.response.payload = response\n
    \n
    [\n  {"user_id":1, "username":"john.doe"},\n  {"user_id":2, "username":"jane.smith"},\n  {"user_id":3,"username":"bob.jones"}\n]\n
    \n

    Explanation:

    \n
      \n
    • The service connects to Oracle using the configured connection.
      • \n
    • It executes a simple SQL query to fetch all user records.
      • \n
    • The result is returned as the service response payload.
      • \n
    \n

    2. Querying a Specific User by ID

    \n

    This service fetches a user by their user_id using a parameterized query. There are multiple ways to retrieve results depending on whether you expect one or many rows.

    \n
    # -*- coding: utf-8 -*-\nfrom zato.server.service import Service\n\nclass GetUserById(Service):\n    """ Service to fetch a user by their user_id.\n    """\n\n    def handle(self):\n\n        # Get the Oracle Database connection from the pool\n        conn = self.out.sql[\'My Oracle DB\']\n\n        # Parameterized SQL to prevent injection\n        query = \'select * from users where user_id = :user_id\'\n\n        # In a real service, this would be read from incoming JSON\n        params = {\'user_id\': 1}\n\n        # Execute the query with parameters; returns a list\n        response = conn.execute(query, params)\n\n        # Set the result as the service\'s response\n        self.response.payload = response\n
    \n
    [{"user_id":1,"username":"john.doe"}]\n
    \n

    Explanation:

    \n
      \n
    • The service expects user_id in the request payload.
      • \n
    • It uses a parameterized query to prevent SQL injection.
      • \n
    • The result is always a list, even if only one row matches.
      • \n
    \n

    3. Calling a Stored Procedure with Input and Output Parameters

    \n

    This service demonstrates how to call an Oracle stored procedure that takes input values and returns output values.

    \n
    # -*- coding: utf-8 -*-\n\n# Zato\nfrom zato.common.oracledb import NumberIn, NumberOut, StringIn, StringOut\nfrom zato.server.service import Service\n\nclass CallProcessData(Service):\n    """ Service to call a stored procedure with input/output params.\n    """\n    def handle(self):\n\n        # Obtain Oracle Database connection\n        conn = self.out.sql[\'My Oracle DB\']\n\n        # Prepare input parameter for NUMBER\n        in_num = NumberIn(333)\n\n        # Prepare input parameter for VARCHAR2\n        in_str = StringIn(\'Hello\')\n\n        # Prepare output parameter for NUMBER (will be written to by the procedure)\n        out_num = NumberOut()\n\n        # Prepare output parameter for VARCHAR2, specify max buffer size (optionally)\n        out_str = StringOut(size=200)\n\n        # Build the parameter list in the order expected by the procedure\n        params = [in_num, in_str, out_num, out_str]\n\n        # Call the stored procedure with the parameters\n        response = conn.callproc(\'process_data\', params)\n\n        # Return the output values as a dictionary in the response\n        self.response.payload = {\n            \'output_num\': out_num.get(),\n            \'output_str\': out_str.get()\n        }\n
    \n
    {"output_num":666, "output_str":"Input was: Hello"}\n
    \n

    Explanation:

    \n
      \n
    • The service prepares input and output parameters using helper classes.
      • \n
    • It calls the process_data procedure with both input and output arguments.
      • \n
    • The result includes both output values, returned as a dictionary.
      • \n
    • Note that you always need to provide the parameters for the procedure in the same order as they were\n declared in the procedure itself.
      • \n
    \n

    4. Calling a Procedure Returning Multiple Rows

    \n

    This service calls a procedure that returns a set of rows (a cursor) and collects the results.

    \n
    # -*- coding: utf-8 -*-\nfrom zato.common.oracledb import RowsOut\nfrom zato.server.service import Service\n\nclass CallGetUsers(Service):\n    """ Service to call a procedure returning a set of rows.\n    """\n    def handle(self):\n\n        # Get Oracle Database connection\n        conn = self.out.sql[\'My Oracle DB\']\n\n        # Prepare a RowsOut object to receive the result set\n        rows_out = RowsOut()\n\n        # Build parameter list for the procedure\n        params = [rows_out]\n\n        # Call the procedure, populating rows\n        conn.callproc(\'get_users\', params)\n\n        # Convert the cursor results to a list of rows\n        rows = list(rows_out.get())\n\n        # Return the list as the service response\n        self.response.payload = rows\n
    \n
    [[1,"john.doe"], [2,"jane.smith"], [3,"bob.jones"]]\n
    \n

    Explanation:

    \n
      \n
    • The service prepares a RowsOut object to receive the rows into. That is, the procedure will write rows into this object.
      • \n
    • It calls the get_users procedure, which populates the rows.
      • \n
    • You call rows_out.get to get the actual rows from the database.
      • \n
    • The rows are converted to a list and returned as the payload.
      • \n
    \n

    4. Returning a Single Object

    \n

    When you know your query will return a single row, you can use conn.one or conn.one_or_none for more predictable results:

    \n
    # -*- coding: utf-8 -*-\nfrom zato.server.service import Service\n\nclass GetSingleUserById(Service):\n    """ # Service to fetch exactly one user or raise if not found/ambiguous.\n    """\n    def handle(self):\n\n        # Get the Oracle Database connection\n        conn = self.out.sql[\'My Oracle DB\']\n\n        # Parameterized SQL query\n        query = \'select * from users where user_id = :user_id\'\n\n        # In a real service, this would be read from incoming JSON\n        params = {\'user_id\': 1}\n\n        # conn.one returns a dict if exactly one row, else raises (zero or multiple rows)\n        result = conn.one(query, params)\n\n        # Return the single user as the response\n        self.response.payload = result\n\nclass GetSingleUserOrNoneById(Service):\n    """ Service to fetch one user, None if not found, or raise an Exception if ambiguous.\n    """\n    def handle(self):\n\n        # Get Oracle Database connection\n        conn = self.out.sql[\'My Oracle DB\']\n\n        # SQL with named parameter\n        query = \'select * from users where user_id = :user_id\'\n\n        # Extract user_id from payload\n        params = {\'user_id\': 1}\n\n        # conn.one_or_none returns a dict if one row, None if zero, raises if multiple rows\n        result = conn.one_or_none(query, params)\n\n        # Return dict or None\n        self.response.payload = result\n
    \n

    Explanation:

    \n
      \n
    • conn.one(query, params) will return a single row as a dictionary if exactly one row is found. If no rows or more than one row are returned, it raises an exception.
      • \n
    • conn.one_or_none(query, params) will return a single row as a dictionary if one row is found, None if no rows are found, but still raises an exception if more than one row is returned.
      • \n
    • Use these methods when you expect either exactly one or zero/one results, and want to handle them cleanly.
      • \n
    \n

    Key Concepts Explained

    \n
      \n
    • Connection Management: Zato handles connection pooling and configuration for you. Use self.out.sql[\'My Oracle DB\'] to get a ready-to-use connection.
      • \n
    • Parameterized Queries: Always use parameters (e.g., :user_id) to avoid SQL injection and improve code clarity.
      • \n
    • Calling Procedures: Use helper classes (NumberIn, StringIn, NumberOut, StringOut, RowsOut) for input/output arguments and recordsets.
      • \n
    • Service Separation: Each service is focused on a single responsibility, making code easier to test and reuse.
      • \n
    \n

    Security and Best Practices

    \n
      \n
    • Always use parameterized queries for user input.
      • \n
    • Manage credentials and connection strings securely (never hardcode them in source code).
      • \n
    • Handle exceptions and database errors gracefully in production code.
      • \n
    • Use connection pooling (Zato does this for you) for efficiency.
      • \n
    \n

    Summary

    \n

    Integrating Oracle Database with Python and Zato services gives you powerful tools for building APIs, automating workflows,\nand connecting enterprise data sources.

    \n

    Whether you need to run queries, call stored procedures,\nor expose Oracle data through REST APIs, Zato provides a robust and Pythonic way to do it.

    \n

    Pandas: Drop Columns By Name in DataFrames

    \n

    \n

    The post Pandas: Drop Columns By Name in DataFrames appeared first on Erik Marsja.

    \n\n

    This blog post will cover Pandas drop columns by name from a single DataFrame and multiple DataFrames. This is a common task when working with large datasets in Python, especially when you want to clean your data or remove unnecessary information. We have previously looked at how to drop duplicated rows in a Pandas DataFrame, and now we will focus on dropping columns by name.

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    Table of Contents

    \n
      \n\n
      • \nHow to use Pandas to drop Columns by Name from a Single DataFrame\n\n
        • \n
      • Dropping Multiple Columns by Name in a Single DataFrame\n\n
        • \n
      • Dropping Columns from Multiple Pandas DataFrames\n\n
        • \n
      • Dropping Columns Conditionally from Panda DataFrame Based on Their Names\n\n
        • \n
      • Summary\n\n
        • \n
      \n
    • Resources\n
    \n\n

    How to use Pandas to drop Columns by Name from a Single DataFrame

    \n\n\n

    The simplest scenario is when we have a single DataFrame and want to drop one or more columns by their names. We can do this easily using the drop() Pandas function. Here is an example:

    \n\n\n
    import pandas as pd\n\n# Create a simple DataFrame\ndf = pd.DataFrame({\n    \'A\': [1, 2, 3],\n    \'B\': [4, 5, 6],\n    \'C\': [7, 8, 9]\n})\n\n# Drop column \'B\' by name\ndf = df.drop(columns=[\'B\'])\n\nprint(df)
    \n\n\n

    In the code chunk above, we drop column ‘B’ from the DataFrame df using the drop() function. We specify the column to remove by name within the columns parameter. The operation returns a new DataFrame with the ‘B’ column removed, and the result is assigned back to df.

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    Compare it to the original dataframe before column ‘B’ was dropped:

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    Dropping Multiple Columns by Name in a Single DataFrame

    \n\n\n

    If we must drop multiple columns simultaneously, we can pass a list of column names to the drop() function. Here is how we can remove multiple columns from a DataFrame:

    \n\n\n
    # Drop columns \'A\' and \'C\'\ndf = df.drop(columns=[\'A\', \'C\'])\n\nprint(df)
    \n\n\n

    In the code above, we removed both columns ‘A’ and ‘C’ from the DataFrame by specifying them in a list. The resulting DataFrame only contains the column ‘B’. Here is the result:

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    Dropping Columns from Multiple Pandas DataFrames

    \n\n\n

    When working with multiple DataFrames, we might want to drop the same columns by name. We can achieve this by iterating over our DataFrames and applying the drop() function to each one.

    \n\n\n
    # Create two DataFrames\ndf1 = pd.DataFrame({\'A\': [1, 2, 3], \'B\': [4, 5, 6], \'C\': [7, 8, 9]})\ndf2 = pd.DataFrame({\'A\': [10, 11, 12], \'B\': [13, 14, 15], \'C\': [16, 17, 18]})\n\n# List of DataFrames\ndfs = [df1, df2]\n\n# Drop column \'B\' from all DataFrames\ndfs = [df.drop(columns=[\'B\']) for df in dfs]\n\n# Print the result\nfor df in dfs:\n    print(df)
    \n\n\n

    In the code chunk above, we first added our two DataFrames, df1 and df2, to a list called dfs to efficiently perform operations on multiple DataFrames at once. Then, using a list comprehension, we drop column ‘B’ from each DataFrame in the list by applying the drop() function to each one. The result is a new list of DataFrames with the ‘B’ column removed from each.

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    Dropping Columns Conditionally from Panda DataFrame Based on Their Names

    \n\n\n

    In some cases, we might not know in advance which columns we want to drop but wish to drop columns based on specific conditions. For instance, we might want to drop all columns that contain a particular string or pattern in their name.

    \n\n\n
    # Drop columns whose names contain the letter \'A\'\ndf = df.drop(columns=[col for col in df.columns if \'A\' in col])\n\nprint(df)
    \n\n\n

    In the code above, we used a list comprehension to identify columns whose names contain the letter ‘A’. We then dropped these columns from the DataFrame.

    \n\n\n

    Summary

    \n\n\n

    In this post, we covered several ways to pandas drop columns by name in both a single DataFrame and across multiple DataFrames. We demonstrated how to remove specific columns, drop multiple columns at once, and even apply conditions for column removal. These techniques are essential for data cleaning and preparation in Python, especially when working with large datasets. By mastering these methods, you can handle your data more efficiently and streamline your data manipulation tasks.

    \n\n\n\n

    Feel free to share this post if you found it helpful, and leave a comment below if you would like me to cover other aspects of pandas or data manipulation in Python!

    \n\n\n

    Resources

    \n\n\n

    Here are some more Pandas-related tutorials:

    \n\n\n\n
      \n
    • Pandas Tutorial: Renaming Columns in Pandas Dataframe
      • \n\n\n\n

      • \n\n\n\n

    Credit: DjangoCon Europe 2025 organizers

    We had a blast at DjangoCon Europe 2025, and hope you did too! This year\xe2\x80\x99s conference brought together brilliant minds from all corners of the globe.     May 14, 2025

    Hugo van Kemenade

    \n\n\n

    PEPs & Co.

    \n

    \n

    PEPs #

    Here’s Barry Warsaw on the origin of\nPEPs, or Python Enhancement Proposals (edited from\nPyBay 2017):

    \n
    \n

    I like backronyms. For those who don’t know: a backronym is where you come up with the\nacronym first and then you come up with the thing that the acronym stands for. And I\nlike funny sounding words, like FLUFL was one of\nthose. When we were working for CNRI, they also ran the IETF conferences. The IETF is\nthe Internet Engineering Task Force, and they’re the ones who come up with the RFCs.\nIf you look at RFC 822, it defines\nwhat an email message looks like.

    \n

    We got to a point, because we were at CNRI we were more intimately involved in the\nIETF and how they do standards and things, we observed at the time that there were so\nmany interesting ideas coming in being proposed for Python that Guido really just\ndidn’t have time to dive into the details of everything.

    \n

    So I thought: well, we have this RFC process, let’s try to mirror some of that so that\nwe can capture the essence of an idea in a document that would serve as a point of\ndiscussion, and that Guido could let people discuss and then come in and read the\nsummary of the discussion.

    \n

    And I was just kind of thinking: well, PEPs, that’s kind of peppy, it’s kind of a\nfunny sounding word. I came up with the word and then I backronymed it into Python\nEnhancement Proposal. And then I wrote PEP 0 and\nPEP 1. PEP 0 was originally handwritten, and so I\nwas the first PEP author because I came up with the name PEP.

    \n

    But the really interesting thing is that you see the E.P. part used in a lot of other\nplaces, like Debian has DEPs now. There’s a lot of other communities that have these\nenhancement proposals so it’s kind of interesting. And then the format of the PEP was\ndirectly from that idea of the RFC’s standard.

    \n

    & Co. #

    Here’s a collection of enhancement proposals from different communities.

    \n\n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n
    AcronymName
    AIPAirflow Improvement Proposals
    AIPAPI Improvement Proposals
    APEAstropy Proposals for Enhancement
    BIPBitcoin Improvement Proposals
    CEPConda Enhancement Proposals
    CFEPconda-forge’s Enhancement Proposals

    \n

    Are there more? Let me know!

    Real Python

    \n\n\n

    How to Get the Most Out of PyCon US

    \n

    \n

    Congratulations! You\xe2\x80\x99re going to PyCon US!

    \n

    Whether this is your first time or you\xe2\x80\x99re a regular attendee, going to a conference full of people who love the same thing as you is always a fun experience. There\xe2\x80\x99s so much more to PyCon than just a bunch of people talking about the Python language\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s a vibrant community event filled with talks, workshops, hallway conversations, and social gatherings. But for first-time attendees, it can also feel a little intimidating. This guide will help you navigate all there is to see and do at PyCon.

    \n

    PyCon US is the biggest conference centered around Python. Originally launched in 2003, this conference has grown exponentially and has even spawned several other PyCons and workshops around the world.

    \n

    Everyone who attends PyCon will have a different experience, and that\xe2\x80\x99s what makes the conference truly unique. This guide is meant to help you, but you don\xe2\x80\x99t need to follow it strictly.

    \n

    By the end of this article, you\xe2\x80\x99ll know:

    \n
      \n
    • How PyCon consists of tutorials, conference, and sprints
      • \n
    • What to do before you go
      • \n
    • What to do during PyCon
      • \n
    • What to do after the event
      • \n
    • How to have a great PyCon
      • \n
    \n

    This guide contains links that are specific to PyCon 2025, but it should be useful for future PyCons as well.

    \n
    \n

    Free Download: Get a sample chapter from Python Tricks: The Book that shows you Python\xe2\x80\x99s best practices with simple examples you can apply instantly to write more beautiful + Pythonic code.

    \n
    \n

    What PyCon Involves

    \n

    Before considering how to get the most out of PyCon, it\xe2\x80\x99s first important to understand what PyCon involves.

    \n

    PyCon is divided into three stages:

    \n
      \n
    1. \n

      Tutorials: PyCon starts with two days of three-hour workshops, during which you learn in depth with instructors. These sessions are worth attending because the class sizes are small, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll have the chance to ask instructors questions directly. You should consider going to at least one of these if you can. They have an additional cost of $150 per tutorial.

      \n
      2. \n
    2. \n

      Conference: Next, PyCon offers three days of talks. Each presentation runs for 30 to 45 minutes, and around five talks run concurrently, including a Spanish-language charlas track. But that\xe2\x80\x99s not all: there are open spaces, sponsors, posters, lightning talks, dinners, and so much more.

      \n
      3. \n
    3. \n

      Sprints: During this stage, you can take what you\xe2\x80\x99ve learned and apply it! This is a four-day exercise where people group up to work on various open-source projects related to Python. If you\xe2\x80\x99ve got the time, going to one or more sprint days is a great way to practice what you\xe2\x80\x99ve learned, become associated with an open-source project, and network with other smart and talented people. If you\xe2\x80\x99re still unconvinced, here\xe2\x80\x99s what to expect at this year\xe2\x80\x99s PyCon US sprints. Learn more about sprints from an earlier year in this blog post.

      \n
      4. \n
    \n

    Since most PyCon attendees go to the conference part, that\xe2\x80\x99ll be the focus of this article. However, don\xe2\x80\x99t let that deter you from attending the tutorials or sprints if you can!

    \n

    You may learn more technical skills by attending the tutorials rather than listening to the talks. The sprints are great for networking and applying the skills you already have, as well as learning new ones from the people you\xe2\x80\x99ll be working with.

    \n

    What to Do Before You Go

    \n

    In general, the more prepared you are for something, the better your experience will be. The same applies to PyCon.

    \n

    It\xe2\x80\x99s really helpful to plan and prepare ahead of time, which you\xe2\x80\x99re already doing just by reading this article!

    \n

    Look through the talks schedule and see which talks sound most interesting. This doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean you need to plan out all of the talks you\xe2\x80\x99ll see in every slot possible. But it helps to get an idea of which topics will be presented so that you can decide what you\xe2\x80\x99re most interested in.

    \n

    Getting the PyCon US mobile app will help you plan your schedule. This app lets you view the schedule for the talks and add reminders for those you want to attend. If you\xe2\x80\x99re having a hard time picking which talks to attend, you can come prepared with a question or problem you need to solve. Doing this can help you focus on the topics that are important to you.

    \n

    If you can, come a day early to check in and attend the opening reception. The line to check in on the first day is always long, so you\xe2\x80\x99ll save time if you check in the day before. There\xe2\x80\x99s also an opening reception that evening, where you can meet other attendees and speakers and check out the various sponsors and their booths.

    \n

    If you\xe2\x80\x99re new to PyCon, the Newcomer Orientation can help you learn about the conference and how you can participate.

    \n

    Read the full article at https://realpython.com/pycon-guide/ \xc2\xbb

    \n
    \n

    [ Improve Your Python With \xf0\x9f\x90\x8d Python Tricks \xf0\x9f\x92\x8c \xe2\x80\x93 Get a short & sweet Python Trick delivered to your inbox every couple of days. >> Click here to learn more and see examples ]

    \nMay 14, 2025 02:00 PM UTC\n

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    Django Weblog

    \n\n\n

    DSF member of the month - Simon Charette

    \n

    \n

    For May 2025, we welcome Simon Charette as our DSF member of the month! \xe2\xad\x90

    \n

    \n

    Simon Charette is a longtime Django contributor and community member. He served on the Django 5.x Steering Council and is part of the Security team and the Triage and Review team. He has been a DSF member since November 2014.
    \nYou can learn more about Simon by visiting Simon\'s GitHub Profile.

    \n

    Let\xe2\x80\x99s spend some time getting to know Simon better!

    \n

    Can you tell us a little about yourself (hobbies, education, etc)

    \n

    My name is Simon Charette and I\'m based in Montr\xc3\xa9al. I\'ve been contributing to Django for over a decade mainly to the ORM and I have a background in software engineering and mathematics. I work as a principal backend engineer at Zapier where we use Python and Django to power many of our backend services. Outside of Django and work I like to spend time cycling around the world, traveling with my partner, and playing ultimate frisbee.

    \n

    Out of curiosity, your GitHub profile picture appears to be a Frisbee, is it correct? If so, have you been playing for a long time?

    \n

    I\'ve been playing ultimate frisbee since college which is around the time I started contributing to Django. It has been a huge part of my life since then as I made many friends and met my partner playing through the years. My commitment to ultimate frisbee can be reflected in my volume of contributions over the past decade as it requires more of my time during certain periods of the year. It also explains why I wasn\'t able to attend most DjangoCon in spring and fall as this is usually a pretty busy time for me. I took part in the world championships twice and I played in the UFA for about 5 years before retiring three years ago. Nowadays I still play but at a lower intensity level and I am focused on giving back to the community through coaching.

    \n

    How did you start using Django?

    \n

    Back in college I was working part time for a web agency that had an in house PHP framework and was trying to determine which tech stack and framework they should migrate to in order to ease onboarding of their developers and reduce their maintenance costs. I was tasked, with another member of the team, to identify potential candidates and despite my lack of familiarity with Python at the time we ended up choosing Django over PHP\'s Symphony mainly because of its spectacular documentation and third-party app ecosystem.

    \n

    What other framework do you know and if there is anything you would like to have in Django if you had magical powers?

    \n

    If I had magical powers I\'d invent Python ergonomics to elegantly address the function coloring problem so it\'s easier for Django to be adapted to an async-ready world. I\'m hopeful that the recent development on the GIL removal in Python 3.13+ will result a renewed interest in the usage of threading, which Django is well equipped to take advantage of, over the systematic usage of an event loop to deal with web serving workloads as the async world comes with a lot of often overlooked drawbacks.

    \n

    What projects are you working on now?

    \n

    I have a few Django related projects I\'m working on mainly relating to ORM improvements (deprecating extra, better usage of RETURNING when available) but the main one has been a tool to keep track of the SQL generated by the Django test suite over time to more easily identity unintended changes that still pass the test suite. My goal with this project is to have a CI invokable command that would run the full Django test suite and provide a set of tests that generated different SQL compared to the target branch so its much easier to identify unintended side effects when making invasive changes to the ORM.

    \n

    Which Django libraries are your favorite (core or 3rd party)?

    \n
      \n
    • DRF
      • \n
    • django-filter
      • \n
    • django-seal (shameless plug)
      • \n
    \n

    What are the top three things in Django that you like?

    \n
      \n
    • The people
      • \n
    • The ORM, unsurprisingly
      • \n
    • The many entry points the framework provides to allow very powerful third-party apps to be used together
      • \n
    \n

    You\'ve contributed significantly to improving the Django ORM. What do you believe is the next big challenge for Django ORM, and how do you envision it evolving in the coming years?

    \n

    The ORM\'s expression interface is already very powerful but there are effectively some remaining rough edges. I believe that adding generalized support for composite virtual fields (a field composed of other fields) could solve many problems we currently face with how relationships are expressed between models as we currently lack a way to describe an expression that can return tuples of values internally. If we had this building block, adding a way to express and compose table expressions (CTE, subquery pushdown, aggregation through subqueries) would be much easier to implement without denaturing the ORM by turning it into a low level query builder. Many of these things are possible today (e.g. django-cte) but they require a lot of SQL compilation and ORM knowledge and can hardly be composed together.

    \n

    How did you start to contribute to the ORM? What would be the advice you have for someone interested to contribute to this field?

    \n

    I started small by fixing a few issues that I cared about and by taking the time to read through Trac, mailing lists, and git-blame for changes in the area that were breaking tests as attempted to make changes. One thing that greatly helps in onboarding on the ORM is to at least have some good SQL fundamentals. When I first started I already had written a MSSQL ORM in PHP which helped me at least understand the idea behind the generation of SQL from a higher level abstraction. Nowadays there are tons of resources out there to help you get started on understand how things are organized but I would suggest this particular video where I attempt to walk through the different phases of SQL generation.

    \n

    Is there anything else you\xe2\x80\x99d like to say?

    \n

    It has been a pleasure to be able to be part of this community for so long and I\'d like to personally thank Claude Paroz for initially getting me interested in contributing seriously to the project.

    \n
    \n

    Thank you for doing the interview, Simon !

    \n

    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n

    eGenix.com

    \n\n\n

    eGenix Antispam Bot for Telegram 0.7.1 GA

    \n

    \n

    Introduction

    \n

    \n

    eGenix has long been running a local user group meeting in D\xc3\xbcsseldorf\ncalled Python Meeting D\xc3\xbcsseldorf and we are using a Telegram group\nfor most of our communication.

    \n\n\n

    In the early days, the group worked well and we only had few spammers\njoining it, which we could well handle manually.

    \n\n

    More recently, this has\nchanged dramatically. We are seeing between 2-5 spam signups per day,\noften at night. Furthermore, the signups accounts are not always easy to\nspot as spammers, since they often come with profile images,\ndescriptions, etc.

    \n\n\n\n

    With the bot, we now have a more flexible way of dealing with the\nproblem.

    \n

    Please see our project page for details and download links.
    \n

    \n

    \n

    \n

    \n

    \n\n\n

    Features

    \n\n
      \n
    • Low impact mode of operation: the bot tries to keep noise in the group\nto a minimum
    • Several challenge mechanisms to choose from, more can be added as\nneeded
    • Flexible and easy to use configuration
    • Only needs a few MB of RAM, so can easily be put into a container or run\non a Raspberry Pi
    • Can handle quite a bit of load due to the async implementation
    • Works with Python 3.9+
      • \n
    • MIT open source licensed
      \n
      • \n
    \n

    News

    \n\n\n

    The 0.7.1 release fixes a few bugs and adds more features:

    \n
      \n
    • Added missing dependency on emoji package to setup (bug introduced in 0.7.0, fixed in 0.7.1)
      \n
      • \n
    • Added user name check for number of emojis, since these are being used a lot by spammers
      • \n
    • Added wheel as requirement, since this is no longer included per default
      • \n
    • Updated copyright year
      • \n
    \n\n
      \n

      It has been battle-tested in production for several years already \nand is proving to be a really useful tool to help with Telegram group \nadministration.
      \n

      \n

      \n

      \n

      More Information

      \n

      For more information on the eGenix.com Python products, licensing and download instructions, please write to sales@egenix.com.

      \n\n

      Enjoy !

      \n

      Marc-Andre Lemburg, eGenix.com

      \n

      \n Django makes it easier to build better web apps more quickly and with less code.\n

      \n

      \n Get started with Django\n

      \n\n
      \n
      \n\n \n\n
      \n\n
      \n
      \n\n \n \n \n\n \n
      \n

      Meet Django

      \n

      \n Django is a high-level Python web framework that encourages rapid development and clean, pragmatic design. Built by experienced developers, it takes care of much of the hassle of web development, so you can focus on writing your app without needing to reinvent the wheel. It\xe2\x80\x99s free and open source.\n

      \n
      \n
      Ridiculously fast.
      \n
      \n

      Django was designed to help developers take applications from concept to completion as quickly as possible.

      \n
      \n\n
      Reassuringly secure.
      \n
      \n

      Django takes security seriously and helps developers avoid many common security mistakes.

      \n
      \n\n
      Exceedingly scalable.
      \n
      \n

      Some of the busiest sites on the web leverage Django\xe2\x80\x99s ability to quickly and flexibly scale.

      \n
      \n
      \n\n Learn more about Django\n
      \n\n
